RATING: 6
Fun little game, but with minimal replay value. (I've played maybe 6-7 times and am pretty done with it). It mainly suffers from the fact that the most effective ways to play are pretty boring (just buy all of the same thing). This is alleviated somewhat by house-ruling the marketplace, but that still doesn't really solve the issue.

Still gets a 6 rating because it's a really nice little gateway/non-gamer/family game. Easy to pitch as "all the fun bits of monopoly without any of the boring bits". Everyone gets it straight away, and actually, for inexperienced gamers who don't immediately see the broken-ness of it, I think it's an interesting puzzle.

RATING: 6
5 plays / 3,4,5 players. (some w/Harbor expansion)

LIKE: Quick and light! The expansion rules to setting up the rows are a must-use in my opinion. It's fun to hit big on a good roll, which reminds me of craps at the casino.

DISLIKE: Bad rolls can slow the game down. The game can be a little boring if people play too nice.

OVERALL: Get the attack-y cards to make it interesting, or pile up on one card to push your luck. Otherwise the game can be dull and frustrating. It should play quickly. The game is very swingy, but oddly entertaining. I like it if played casually.

RATING: 5
*Rating based on one play of a PnP copy.

I had heard a lot of buzz surrounding this game, so I was interested in giving it a try.  It was quickly clear to me that this is not a game for me.  There's just too much randomness, too much screwage, and not enough decisions in the game.

+Fun artwork and theme

-Lots of stealing from other players.  Normally I would put this as neither a positive nor a negative since some players like this kind of thing, but in this game I think it's just a negative.  With all the cards that take money from your opponents, you pretty much have to spend all your money as soon as you can, or else risk losing it, which I think detracts from the decision making in the game.
-Highly luck based.
-Has a snowballing, rich-get-richer issue.
-Takes a lot of table space.

A sim city builder that involves drafting cards that represent different parts of your city that produce a certain amount of currency or special power based on a certain die roll. More tactical than strategic this game is fast and light but with just enough decision making to keep even "eurosnoots" interested, min maxers min maxing, and AP players overwhelmed. Always worth the real estate in your bag of games on game night.

The base game is good but some buildings are over powered in my opinion but I can't wait for the expansion which is supposed to solve this issue. I feel like this is a great game to play with people that don't play games much or are new to the hobby. Easy for all ages with minimal help for younger players. A must try unless your group is heavy gamers only, then you might want to pass on it before you try it.

Quite a fun little engine-building game.  The similarities to [b]Settlers[/b] end at 2d6 distribution.  It is random, sure, but you can "bet" so many ways on the die rolls: 1 die, 2 dice, bet on yourself, bet on others, multiply buildings or diversify.  It's a simple, yet clever design that deserves its popularity, not unlike [b]Splendor[/b].

RATING: 7.5
I played this game just three times, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

Machi Koro is one of those games that got quite a deal of buzz when it came out. However it never really caught my eye, probably because I'm not a big fan of its artwork for some reason. When I finally got to try it though, I definitively understood why this game is so popular.

Machi Koro is in fact one of the best light/family games I've ever played. It's super simple to explain, it plays smoothly but it still offers a good amount of decisions, strategies and tactics. The game is also very engaging, because what other people roll on their turn might affect you as well. This is all so very clever! Yet my favorite thing about it is the fact that you can unlock special powers, as you build the big buildings. This adds a level of depth to the game-play, in which you need to decide what special ability you need to unlock first.

Machi Koro is a great title for those situations where you need a filler game... and it also comes in a quite portable box. DO NOT however play it with the expansions, because they ruin everything that is so fun about it. They just make the game more convoluted and stupidly random. The game is a very well put-together product as it is and it's one that I will enjoy playing every now and then.

Rating with the expansions: 3.5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First, the negatives: a dumbed-down version of Settlers of Catan. Strategic thought is necessary, yes; but the strategies are pretty obvious (and by-the-by, playing with either one die or two can be game-winning: it all depends on the cards you've got). It's an engine-building game; but it is so stinkin' short that game-end leaves one feeling like, "Wow...I was just getting started, and now it's over." There is a definitely a runaway leader problem.

But in spite of all that, it's fun! Machi Koro is the perfect game to play with non-gamers. And, in spite of my complaint about its brevity, being such a short game is a plus, because you can then play something better!

RATING: 6
Machi Koro is a fast-paced game for 2-4 players. Each player wants to develop the city on his own terms in order to complete all of the landmarks under construction faster than his rivals. On his turn, each player rolls one or two dice. If the sum of the dice rolled matches the number of a building that a player owns, he gets the effect of that building; in some cases opponents will also benefit from your die (just as you can benefit from theirs). Then, with money in hand a player can build a landmark or a new building, ideally adding to the wealth of his city on future turns. The first player to construct all of his landmarks wins!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

But the base game is virtually the same every time you play it, unfortunately. If you add the expansion, I would call it a good game. But the base game on its own is just okay.

RATING: 6
The game that I would say it most closely competes with would be Catan. If you're trying to decide between those games, definitely choose this one.

But for me, it's pretty unexceptional. It's a variant on Dominion (sort-of), but with sufficient dice action to make it winnable by almost anyone. I generally like games with a race-to-the-target ending, and I appreciate how easy this game should be to teach (though the guy who taught it to us was impressively tongue-tied in his attempt to do so). I can see why it's popular. I'd play it again. But I'd rather play Carcassonne, Metropolys, or Junk Art if I wanted something fun to play with people who don't play games and want something they can learn in 2 minutes.

RATING: 6
As everyone says, Machi Koro is reminiscent of both Settlers (roll a dice and collect resources - well just one, coins) and Dominion (buy new cards).  The aim is to be the first to build four costly advanced buildings.  There is a big dollop of dicey luck, but this is a quick filler so we'll not worry too much about that. A more serious criticism is that there just aren't many card types and therefore few strategic options.  Yeah, but this is a quick filler and quite fun.

Production quality is decent while the artwork is nothing special.

RATING: 6
Απλό καρτοπαίχνιδο με ζάρια που κινείτε πάνω στον βασικό μηχανισμό των Αποίκων. Κάθε γύρα ρίχνεις ένα ζάρι και ανάλογα με το αποτέλεσμα ενεργοποιούνται κάποιες κάρτες και παράγουν λεφτά με τα οποία μπορείς να αγοράσεις καινούργιες κάρτες ώστε μελλοντικά να έχεις μεγαλύτερο εισόδημα. Νικητής είναι όποιος καταφέρει πρώτος να χτίσει και τις 4 monument κάρτες του.

Ουσιαστικά είναι ένα engine building παιχνίδι αλλά η υπερβολική χρήση του ζαριού το κάνει πολύ luck dependant. Οι κανόνες είναι εξαιρετικά απλοί και ο ρυθμός είναι γρήγορος. Μια παρτίδα 4άρων παικτών μας πήρε 45 λεπτά. 

Υπάρχουν διάφορα κτήρια/κάρτες που μπορείς να αγοράσεις. Άλλες λειτουργούν μόνο στη γύρα σου, άλλες μόνο στις γύρες των αντιπάλων και άλλες πάντα, όμως γενικά πιο σημαντικό είναι να έχεις το ζάρι με το μέρος σου. 

Απλό, γρήγορο, εύκολο και με χαριτωμένα γραφικά. Κατάλληλο για casual παίκτες αλλά δεν βλέπω πως μπορεί να  ικανοποιήσει τους hardcorάδες.

Time: 30 mins
Dice rolling, city building

Welcome to the city of Machi Koro, the Japanese card game that is sweeping the world. You've just been elected Mayor.  Congrats! Unfortunately the citizens have some pretty big demands: jobs, a theme park, a couple of cheese factories and maybe even a radio tower.  A tough proposition since the city currently consists of a wheat field, a bakery and a single die. Armed only with your trusty die and a dream, you must grow Machi Koro into the largest city in the region.  You will need to collect income from developments, build public works and steal from your neighbor's coffers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A worthy gateway game that might be considered in the family of Settlers of Cataan in that it allows players to roll the dice on their turn, and have their roll affect every other player's fortunes, which of course, is a desirable design feature to keep everyone interested in every roll.  This attribute explains, in part, the success of Settlers.

The design also cleverly includes a progression from rolling one die to two, and card capabilities/rewards split between those that apply when the player rolls, and those that apply when other players roll.

Is this a gamers' game that I am going to want to play often?  No, not really.  Nevertheless, this game might serve nicely as a gateway game, and please a lot of non-gamers.  I certainly prefer this dice roller to Yahtzee, and I might even prefer it to Settlers at this point in my game playing history.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
A light engine-building filler with good player interaction and fun artwork that plays pretty fast.

I find that the engines are a bit too obvious. In Dominion, a different set of cards will come out in every game, making each game different. In this game, all the cards are used, so in theory, you can use the same strategy every time. This also hurts the game's replayability. Some strategies seem stronger than others, and I don't really see a reason to buy cards that only give you money on your turn when you can buy cards that can give you money on anyone's turn.

RATING: 7.5
An engaging little dice and card game, where your dice roll generates income from your or your opponents cards.  Income is used to acquire more cards.  Each card has a trigger number similar to Settlers.  The winner is the first to complete (buy) their four objective cards, one of which allows you to upgrade from one dice to two, with the knock on changes to the probabilities of rolling numbers in the 1 (none) to 12 (1 in 36) range.  I played the 7 strategy which failed first game and won the second.  A nice filler - if only I could get hold of a copy from somewhere!

RATING: 6.5
Machi Koro is the epitome of the filler: plays in 30 minutes, light but with some choices (when to switch to two die; spreading numbers vs bet on some for big turns), apparently random but with lots of odds management...

Production quality is definitely pleasant: great tin box, huge clunky dice, thick cardboard money. The only sub par component are the cards: they are flimsy, but are not to be shuffled, so who cares?

The game plays at its full potential with 4 players, it is still fun and ok with 3, but definitely lacks some excitement with only 2.
Rating is probably 7 with more players, but it loses at least a half point for playing poorly with 2...

I am eager to play it with my gamer friends as the closing filler, as well as with non gamers and my daughters.

RATING: 7
An interesting, quick game that is a good combination of luck as well as strategy (Which card(s) do you want to buy?  When should you roll one or two dice?).  However, we've found, when playing with two players, the incentive to roll two dice is minimal, thus reducing the game to who is going to roll a 4 (the "convenience store" number) first.  The game is also not typically interesting after this point, as the lucky player is virtually unbeatable.

Game is more interesting with 3 or 4 players, as the incentive to roll two dice and diversify what cards you own is much greater.

RATING: 5
Machi Koro is a tableau-building, dice-rolling, engine-creating card game, wherein you build up a little town of building cards, and each building does something on a certain die roll. You can try to cover all possible dice roll outcomes, or you can focus on just a few numbers that will offer big payouts, but after all's said and done, the luck of the dice is going to be the deciding factor. I've played it twice and I feel like I've seen absolutely all there is to see here.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I like the simplicity of this game, combined with the variety of strategies you can attempt. It was funny because no matter how strategic I thought I was being, plans always seemed to backfire when it came to the roll of the dice. Yet all of us were close to victory at the same time. I'll be interested to play this game some more in order to explore what strategies are most effective. It can get a little nasty particularly when you start stealing stuff from your opponents, but since it's luck of the dice most people probably won't take it personal. Now that we've played once I hope that the game will go quicker and we'll be able to enjoy it in a more reasonable amount of time. This is a keeper.

RATING: 9
Initial Impressions:

Really fun mix of Dominion / Catan.  The base game is good by itself, but will feel "samey" quickly.  The expansion is a must for this game, and the best way to play is where 10 types are available per turn from a deck of building cards.

Update 1:
Dropped to an 8 (from a 9), because I feel like the only way to really play the game is with 3 people.  4 player, the red cards are too powerful, and in 2 player they are too weak.

Update 2:
I think the Machi Koro expansion fixes the issues I had with the previous expansion.  I only will play with the variant of randomly having 10 buildings available at any time, however with the Machi Koro exp, this variant was even more fun!  This is one of my favorite games, and even though it has a ton of luck, I have so much fun playing every time we bust it out.

RATING: 4
This is an ultra-light roll-and-buy game. Roll dice, get or pay money, maybe buy a card. Really, there's not much strategy to be had at all, simple maths tells you which cards are the best.
Plus, the game drags and drags and drags, because many rounds, you'll roll, your number doesn't come up, you get to do nothing. It's really not the 30 minutes on the box, more like 60, and each and every one of them is boring and tedious.
The only upside I see to this game is that it's realistically age 6 and up, so if you want a gateway game for kids, this might be it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
It's been a while since I've played a game that made me this angry, and in returning to this rating I've actually dropped it lower; now the challenge is to try to avoid ranting and raving uselessly and stay constructive. 

The luck in this game is infuriating. Part of that might be that the Take That style cards should just be out of the game... but even without them, this game's resource system is like Catan. That's the only description I can use, and most people would agree that the resource system is the most outdated and infuriating part of Catan. But in this game... it's nearly the whole game. I hate how much power the luck has here. And yes, I acknowledge, that if I make a bad city, it's my fault, and I may lose from that. But the game also kicks you when your down, and rewards one random player amongst the three that have built a good city. When I lose it feels like you've failed to crawl out of a tar pit, and worse than that, when you win, it feels like you mised the win on a lucky roll anyways. I feel bad when I'm losing, I feel bad when I win (oops! Sorry, I guess I have enough money to win now), I feel bad watching other people losing, this game is just WAY too full of feel bads. Not denying that there are fun parts to this game, but there are way better tableau builders than this. I will attempt to never play this again.

RATING: 5
A pretty light engine building game of building up a tableau of cards that trigger on die rolls.  Most cards earn you money when triggered, which you can use to buy more cards.  Your final goal is to build a set of 4 cards (each player is given an identical set of these 4 cards at the beginning of the game, on their unbuilt side).

I typically enjoy engine building games, but for some reason this one just felt kind of ho-hum to me.  However, it works really well as a filler that has a little but of meat to it since you can pick a strategy and roll with it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Quite light. Quite. I enjoy it, but I'm not sure if it's because the game is all that good as a goofy little engine builder, or if it's just that I enjoy calling out "Everybody wins!" on certain die rolls.

However, the Harbour expansion makes the game much worse for me. I won't play with it ever again.

Why?

Well, it adds many many red and purple cards which don't add money to the game, they simply pass it along from one player to the next. Not adding money on die rolls slows the game down (as does adding a fifth player and more mandatory buildings needed to win). The game is a fun thirty minute game. It is a very very long game when it takes over an hour (or worse, over two hours).

The random release of buildings makes this now more of a tactical game instead of a strategic game. It also, adds luck to the game, in that some players will never get a chance to buy the good cards.

It also can result in a very slow game if the purchasable cards are all late game cards (7 or higher) and you are in the early game (it happened to me...), or half of a combo cards (you know the ones, the cards that aren't good on their own).

My long winded way of saying, that the game increases the luck, and adds frustration, while at least doubling, more likely trippling, and occasionally quadrupling, the play time.

Based on 1 play with 4 players:

I am not really as enamored with this one as I thought I would be. It's cute enough, but the combo-building the game relies on just wasn't really doing it for me. I feel like I'd rather just play Dominion, etc.?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
i don't know what it is with me and Japanese games. They're charming in their way; Machi Koro especially so, with its cartoonish card art and general breezy approach. But this one's just too lightweight for my tastes. MK technically rewards strategy in the sense that the player who approaches the game with some sort of plan will outdo the player who moves totally at random, but beyond it being worthwhile to have that some sort of plan in place it seems to me that the dice determine winners and losers.

Dominion is a similar game in some senses, but what makes Dominion a far superior game to MK, in my opinion, is three things: one is that Dominion's kingdom cards vary from game to game and thus no two games play out the same. The second is that Dominion's varied kingdom cards have real (but non-obvious) synergies between them that work out in ways that surprise and delight the player. The third: one essential aspect of the Dominion experience is that success in VP gathering ends up being a drag on the means of future success, since most green cards are useless in game. Machi Koro is strictly win-to-win/snowball effect.

Part of the problem may be that I experienced MK in a period when I was obsessing over Mottainai (similar playtime) and Suburbia (superficially similar theme), both of which are far superior gamer's games than Machi Koro is or (to be fair) even tries to be. 

But mostly it's just not what I'm looking for in a game. I was not expecting something super-deep but I know that it's possible for a game to be both mechanically simple and strategically interesting. Machi Koro [i]could be[/i] but [i]isn't[/i].

RATING: 7.5
[b]AVOID THE RULES[/b] ([COLOR=#FF0000][b]A V[/b][/COLOR]ideo [COLOR=#FF0000][b]O[/b][/COLOR]f [COLOR=#FF0000][b]I[/b][/COLOR]nstruction & [COLOR=#FF0000][b]D[/b][/COLOR]emonstration [COLOR=#0000FF][b]T[/b][/COLOR]hat [COLOR=#0000FF][b]H[/b][/COLOR]elps [COLOR=#0000FF][b]E[/b][/COLOR]ase [COLOR=#FF0000][b]R[/b][/COLOR]ule [COLOR=#FF0000][b]U[/b][/COLOR]nderstanding & [COLOR=#FF0000][b]L[/b][/COLOR]earning - [COLOR=#FF0000][b]E[/b][/COLOR]asy [COLOR=#FF0000][b]S[/b][/COLOR]tart!)

[floatright][imageid=2005702][/floatright][u]BGG:[/u] http://boardgamegeek.com/video/45290/machi-koro/machi-koro-avoid-the-rules-by-connect-more
[u]YouTube:[/u] https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=omTGSKilWlI

[thing=143884][/thing] is a very welcome, light, bone tossing filler with some interesting strategies that range from 'all in' to 'hedging your bets'. 

This game may be, naturally, compared to [thing=13][/thing] in the sense that they are both [b]dice rolling[/b], then collect resources/income based on the dice roll games. Rather than expanding your network on a board (like Settlers), each player in Machi Koro drafts cards ([b]card drafting[/b]) from a common pool and adds in to their own tableau. The cards drafted help the players earn income throughout the game, which is required to construct the necessary buildings to win.

Playing [thing=143884][/thing] is like being at a hot table of Craps in Vegas with lots of group cheering and upbeat energy!

RATING: N/A
SA3
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
I'd heard a lot of buzz for this game, so felt I had to try it out and ended up getting it as a birthday gift from my brother. It's a game that was right up our alley.

The game itself is relatively simple - roll the dice, collect income from your buildings that match what you rolled, and then buy a new building if you want (and can afford it). Eventually you try to earn enough to be the first to build 4 landmarks.

There is a lot of chance here, and it's really more a game about gambling than strategy. But it's a lot of fun if you just expect that you might have a string of bad luck.  A fun game for families, but not for the risk-averse.

RATING: 7
Build your city by buying new cards from a panel of 10 different types of buildings. Each building has a specific financial effect. Some cards generate money in your own turn, others in anyone's turn, and yet others in individual other players' turns. Players depend on the throw of the dice, which can lead to some frustrating rounds in which one can't do anything.

RATING: 7
When I first heard about this game, I was eager to get it, so I ordered it at my local store.  After I bought it, opened it, and read the rules, I was a little disappointed. I guess I was expecting more?  Then I played it.  It was a lot of fun.  I'm partial to engine building/economic snowball games, so I really enjoyed it.  I can't wait to play it more.  I didn't feel like the decisions were obvious, although the overall strategy of only rolling one die seemed to jump out, but I doubt this will be dominant as we play the game more and try other things.  I can see this getting old rather quickly, as the short play time will see this getting table frequently until it is "played out."  But I've read that there are at least a couple of expansions so this might alleviate that problem if it should ever occur.

An added plus, I can play it with the kids.  They can grasp the concepts, and the length is within their attention span.

RATING: 4
[15 Feb 2017] - 4 (1 play)
Damn the dice to hell. This game is totally about getting lucky when activating your cards. In our 4 player game, I built what I thought would have gotten income but didn't. Making matters worse, I ended up always rolling a 3 to pay everyone else at the table on my turn. Not too much later, I find myself with no money and no one rolling my numbers of a single six sided die. Without any way to work out of the hole, I was forced to endure the rest of this luck fest until someone one. Would not recommend unless your idea of gaming is a Vegas slot machine.

RATING: 1
I honestly think with optimal play the game is just horribly broken and takes waaaaay longer than the advertised 30 minutes.

There are so many rolls where no income is generated, and instead it is just passed around among players.  No one dares save $$ across rounds because it just gets stolen, so this drags the game on because it's a generally bad strategy to save up for city upgrades.  

The strategy is paper thin, and while there is nothing wrong with that, this game can be painfully long.  The harbor expansion can make this already broken game even longer. 

Play Kings of Tokyo instead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Everyone loves this game. Not a ton of depth but very fun dice-chucking, money-making action.

EDIT: Will probably drop below a 6 with further plays. Way too long for a game that is almost entirely luck. Really brings home the fact that in a small sample dice will not conform to probability. There will be way 'too many' of some numbers and very few of others. This will often lead to a run away leader problem.

Most sessions of MK are extremely boring.

It's ok with kids if you take out the two most expensive objectives to keep the length down.

Edit: There are much better games to play with kids. I'm glad the average on this has finally fallen below 7. I hope people continue to revise their ratings after more plays. MK makes a great first impression but soon becomes remarkably tedious. Glaring design flaws (clogging, runaway leaders, seemingly endless loop of money passing in the late game) quickly become apparent and have not been fixed with expansions. A very cute, very bad game.

Hopefully Card Kingdom of Valeria, Dice City, or another of the imitators will find a cleaner implementation of the 'shared dice/tableau activation' concept.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Adorable little die rolling, money making game.  Players on their turn roll a die, which triggers income production off held cards (in Settlers of Catan fashion).  Every player can possibly earn income on any player's turn.  The player who rolled the die then may purchase a new card to add to their portfolio, or one of their four Landmark cards - which grant a power for the remainder of the game.

Brilliant little filler, instantly addictive and leaves you hungry for more.  Some tactics and strategies to explore too.

RATING: 8
A very nice engine-building filler with excellent use of colors and art.   Will inevitably be reprinted and made widely available, but I hope that the art and look of the game remains untouched.   Dice rolling is used to generate money to buy cards from a center pool (shades of Dominion).   There is a fair amount of money stealing and the it takes up a lot of table space for such a quick and simple game - those are my only two minor complaints.   An unexpected hit for me.   It strips all of the work out of engine building and leaves behind only the fun.   (I'm surprised how many people compare this to Settlers.  I see why, but find that misleading).   

The 10-Pile variant is good for mixing the game up, and thwarting dominant strategies and 1-track-minded players.  Maybe adds a little length to the game but can be worth it in that situation.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Machi Koro is a strange beast. The game is random on extreme levels, your victory entirely decided by the dice. Your decisions are generally split into two broad categories: reduce risk for minimal reward or increase risk for greater reward, the former being more successful unless the latter pulls off a number of lucky rolls.

Despite this, there is an undeniable level of fun to the game. Perhaps it's the playful art style or the satisfaction of setting up your city so that you win very big on a certain roll and then getting that roll three times in a row. Depth may be minimal, but you'll still find some goodness.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This rocks (but only with the Harbour Expansion for more than one or two replays). A true gateway game and everyone I've introduced it to has loved it. Doesn't get a huge amount of big ups from reviewers but you need to play it a few times to really get the depth of what's going on. And the theme and mechanics integrate beautifully. Would be a 9 with the Harbour Expansion.

RATING: 5
First play of the base game was with the Harbor expansion, and that gets a rating of 4. Rating of 5 is a guess based on how it would work without the expansion. Engine-building snowball problems appear due to limits on interaction. Tactcial decisions aren't rewarded at the end of your turn. Strategic decisions are rewarded randomly. The harbor expansion's 10-stack limit means some players will get uneven access to the cards they want, leading to a stalled engine.

RATING: 8
Machi Koro. WOW! What a game. Sometimes at Essen there is hype generated by scarcity (this year 2013 with Patchistory), sometimes there is hype generated by something innovative (Tzolk'in), sometimes by a near-universally acclaimed great game (Terra mystica), and sometimes, just sometimes, all three join together into an unstoppable gaming phenomena which takes the world by storm (think Love Letter). I would not be in any way surprised if Machi Koro follows this path. It is a gem. 

Part Settlers (dice determine the cards which are actioned), part Dominion (buildings are bought and form a deck but which is constantly in play via your tableau), part The City, but actually none of these as it feels so refreshingly new. 

It plays in 30 minutes max but time flies by and so it feels really really fast. And there is always something to do: on others' turns you may be taking money from the bank if their roll activates your building, or you may be stealing from them, and on your turn a whole host of actions become possible as your city grows. Yes it is true that the outcome is determined by dice, but you determine which dice rolls work in your city and you manage your own probabilities. And after playing a couple of games you realise how cleverly the card numbers and probabilities are worked out. 

I could go on but suffice to say it is a game which brings a smile to the face just thinking about it. Nothing I can think of packs so much punch into so short a time. And that's enough for me. One final comment: this will get picked up by a major publisher but let's just hope we get to see the lovely fresh artwork and that it isn't ruined in a Trains or Coup fashion.

RATING: 8
Machi Koro is a fun light-weight tableau building game where players are competing to build up their cities the fastest.  There is a lot of luck in the game as dice rolls pretty much decide what is going to happen.  

However, there is enough decision making in the game as players have a choice in terms of what cards they want to buy to combo with the other available cards and best cover the dice rolls.  Players also must decide if they want to progress to rolling 2 dice or stay at 1.    

I truly feel the game benefits tremendously with the Harbor expansion or should at least be played with the  setup style in the expansion (starting with 10 unique stacks of buildings).  This way the available buildings at the start are always different, thus changing up the available strategies each game.  

The game plays in around an hour and although it may start a little bit slower, the dice rolls near the end are always highly anticipated, and are often accompanied with cheers and groans.  If luck doesn't bother you and you aren't looking for something deeper, this game is highly recommended!

RATING: 6
Quick, light, easy to teach game with appealing graphics.  Few interesting strategy decisions; may be too luck-driven for some tastes.  Not all cards offer the same expected return for the price; possibly a game balance issue for the math impaired; also a fair number of cards seem very unbalanced.  A good gateway game or filler. Rating a 5 without the expansion; with the Harbor expansion, 6.

Update: Arrggh!! Arrggh!! Arrggh!!  Too much $@&^*#)% luck!!  Update update:  you can play well or you can play poorly, but the dice will determine whether you win.  Frustratingly luck dependent.  Further update:  EGAD!  This game can be extremely frustrating due to the luck factor.  Strongly considering trading.  Arrggh!

Update update update:  I've relaxed a bit.  This game can be fun once you accept that die rolls can defy probability for a long time.

Without the harbor, try randomly populating the market before the game by rolling a die for each type of establishment; on a 1-2 place one fewer than the number of players, on a 3-4 place establishments equal to the number of players, and on a 5-6 place one more than the number of players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Welcome to the city of Machi Koro. You've just been elected Mayor. Congrats! Unfortunately the citizens have some pretty big demands: jobs, a theme park, a couple of cheese factories and maybe even a radio tower. A tough proposition since the city currently consists of a wheat field, a bakery and a single die.

Armed only with your trusty die and a dream, you must grow Machi Koro into the largest city in the region. You will need to collect income from developments, build public works, and steal from your neighbors' coffers. Just make sure they aren't doing the same to you!

Machi Koro is a fast-paced game for 2-4 players. Each player wants to develop the city on his own terms in order to complete all of the landmarks under construction faster than his rivals. On his turn, each player rolls one or two dice. If the sum of the dice rolled matches the number of a building that a player owns, he gets the effect of that building; in some cases opponents will also benefit from your die (just as you can benefit from theirs). Then, with money in hand a player can build a landmark or a new building, ideally adding to the wealth of his city on future turns. The first player to construct all of his landmarks wins!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I have only played with the integrated harbour expansion which I believe changes the game significantly as cards are shuffled together and there is a river of 10 to buy from rather than a tableau of cards a la dominion style.

Actually I quite like this. The four card types (blue, green, red and purple) are actually quite clever and especially the red (steal card) and blue (make money) cards trigger in any turn when their number is rolled therefore you do watch what other people are doing.

The end game race to build condition feels a little forced but I am not sure what other mechanic for end game could have been added as there are plenty of cards and we only saw a fraction of them so playing through the deck would drag this out considerably.

2nd play: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:

2p - Not interesting.
4p - Too long: coins get stolen often.

1st play: DEC/14. I read the rules and knew what to expect, but even with that prep, this game was lighter than I expected. Go for a nice spread of numbers or focus on a few with big payouts. I really need to try this again with less than 4p. 4p wasn't much fun even within the context of the game.

I feel that with the expansion and the 10-card draft, this game would be much more to my tastes. At that point though, it's a $45 (online prices!) game.

3rd play: DEC/14. Lowered rating to one star. Without an expansion and draft mode, this game isn't even interesting as filler.

RATING: 7
I'm fascinated that a few folks rate this game a perfect "10."    There is just so little game here.  I love the production values and the game is a fun, light simplified Catan.   I give it a "7" instead of a 5 or 6 because of the production values and because my 6 yr old daughter likes it and can play reasonably well as I try to move her from "kids games" to "real games."  MK is a nice step in that direction.    

BTW, this game is not a "Deck Builder."  You only have the cards you have purchased in your tableau and then you roll dice and buy more.   This game is much more closely related to a self-published game called "In the City: Origins."   If you like this sort of Tableau builder then you should check out "In the City" --as there is much more strategy and no dice.  (one downside- the production values of "In the City" are quite low, with public domain art that sometimes doesn't seem to really have anything to do with the particular card it is illustrating).

Im Grunde ist MACHI KORI ein komprimiertes, auf den Kern von MONOPOLY gebrachtes MONOPOLY: man latscht mit dem Würfel auf Häuser (Karten) bekommt Geld (Miete) oder zahlt Geld (Miete) und versucht über den Kauf von Karten (Häusern) seine Einnahmen (Miete) zu erhöhen. RATING: 5
It's a light, fun game. Play it with your non-gamer friends. There are some different strategies to try out but don't expect too much of it.

I recommend playing this with slightly different rules. Instead of having all cards available, make three decks. One contains all cards that activate on 1-6, one all cards that activate on 7-14 and one all purple ones. Draw from each deck until you have two different purple cards and 5 (4 if you're playing 1v1) different from the other two decks available (put multiple copies of a card in one pile). This is your market place. Refill up to the same amount of different cards as needed.

Without the expansion this will get boring FAST.

PS: The table space needed for this tiny game is RIDICULOUS.

RATING: 4
For sale: https://boardgamegeek.com/geekmarket/product/786214

Within a few plays this becomes transparent as a race for a starter set that will give you something on almost every pip first. Toss in one or two red "take that" cards to slow the opponents, then race for combo sets... and cross fingers. 

Any perceived "strategy" where you are banking on one particular range or combo of pips will mostly fail and occasionally pay off (so basically it's an illusion of a strategy). That's it.  All of the randomness of Catan in less time.  Yay?

Particularly boring with two players. 

I wish some of the gushing video reviewers of this game had played it more than a couple of times before praising the emperor's new clothes quite so much (I'm looking at you Shut Up & Sit Down). It's just ok, in my opinion. I would play [thing=70323][/thing] over this any day.

The Harbor expansion is not a panacea for the mediocrity of the core game. It takes the randomness and cranks it up to 11 with a random building market, transforming a mostly innocuous humdrum game from amusing trash to intolerably drawn out, pseudo-strategic trash.

Expansions/Promos owned:

[thing=143789][/thing][/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.75
The theme of Machi Koro is ostensibly city building, but I would argue that die rolling is its own theme in board gaming, and this applies here.  You certainly won't feel like you just grew your city by adding a bakery, for example --it's just another roll to get income (similar to Settlers of Catan). 

While the luck of die rolling is by far the biggest element to this light filler, there are statistically several areas that make the game more interesting than just rolling dice (for example, "upgrading" your city to roll two dice, changing both the distribution and available outcomes.  No amount of skill will overcome luck though, so if you tend to either: dislike rolling dice to do well, or dislike luck playing a large part in determining the winner, you may want to avoid this.

If you know much about statistics, you may find yourself actually calculating the value of cards under each path (one die/two dice) to optimize your buy pattern (assuming you get income from your die rolls).  However, because individual players may make suboptimal decisions, a player can hurt themselves while hurting you.

The quality of the components is below average, but not terribly so.  There's also a lot of air in the box, perhaps making you think there's more to the game than just a deck of cards, 2 dice, and some money tokens.  Or maybe it's an attempt to make room for expansions, turning this light filler into a potentially more expensive and profitable game.

If you enjoy rolling dice to find out if you succeed,  or playing around with probabilities to optimize strategy, you might find this game a bit addictive.  There aren't a lot of cards or strategic possibilities, but this is the first game I've seen take the approach of adding dice to change the distribution of outcomes, potentially for all the players.  As a filler, I think it succeeds, although I wish the cards were a little sturdier, there were more interesting cards to buy, and the strategy more developed.

As anything more than a filler, I think it fails, simply because die rolling, especially early in building up your engine, really makes the strategic aspect minimal in comparison to the luck.  As an all ages game that can include younger children, I think this is good.

RATING: 7.2
I previously felt the game was broken—really after my first and second plays. Despite that, I bought the game because of its beautiful motifs and because the gameplay was adequate.

That said, I tired of it because of what I considered to be a Halifax Hammer-type game breaking strategy. In my first games, I purchased as many gear-symbol cards as I could and then bought as many factory cards as I could. As soon as I or someone else upgraded to 2 dice, I was able to occasionally generate a lot of revenue (I remember receiving 42 money in one die roll once).

I think I may have mistakenly given Machi Koro short shrift. With strong players, the game balances much more easily as I found out yesterday (7/29/2015). Also, the expansion does use some randomization to ensure a different balance and gameplay style is necessary each time the game hits the table.

Good game.

Machi Koro is a really fun game to play a few times. Having only played twice, I already get the sense of sameness and the need for more and more expansions. Not in a good way, (because you love it so much). More in the way that it would start to feel very stale without them.

RATING: 4
Nice concept and art, but promised more fun than it delivered, mainly because early luck is important, and it's very much a "rich get richer" game where an early lead can become unstoppable. There is no balancing mechanism for players at an early disadvantage to recover, and no way for players to effectively gang up on a runaway leader either. So, overall, not a well designed game. I'm not saying this from sour grapes because I was the runaway leader in this game ...  One other player could do nothing on three consecutive turns, another player had his (very few) coins stolen which deprived him of the opportunity to buy buildings. Note: this comment is based on a single play with only the base game. But I can't be doing with games that are badly designed from the outset and require an expansion to play well, so will be unlikely to play again with the expansions to see if it plays better.

RATING: 7.5
A light-medium game which has a clean mechanic.  There is a bit of "take that" (Coffee and purple buildings).  Players gradually build up their Town, and the first to complete the construction of 4 pre-set buildings (everyone has the same 4) wins.

There is some randomness, but it can be mitigated by the cards you purchase, which trigger on different numbers, and may or not not trigger on other players turns.

A nice filler to end a game day, or while you wait for players to arrive.  30-45 minutes.

RATING: 4
4-6-15
I don't like games that try to be both strategic yet benefit from directly screwing other people over.  Let alone that these screwing-over decisions are not made strategically, or even tactically, but rather by a roll of the dice.  There is no way to really mitigate your luck, no catch-up mechanisms, and a nasty runaway leader problem.

Stone Age mitigates luck by letting you assign the number of dice as well as the target resource.  Most Euros for that matter allow you to make tactical decisions that can be risky but pay off handsomely.  Any choice you try to make here is reliant upon lucky or unlucky dice rolls, and I would guess that in most cases, the first person to get a shopping mall is going to be the victor.

Felt like Ameritrash without the fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Machi Koro is a cute little, dice driven game that has some strategy as well as luck.

Players roll, and cards matching the roll activate, generally providing money to the rolling player, but sometimes to all players.

There are certain combos which are more effective, with good rolls, so players will want to watch anyone aiming for a fools mate and build leetches.

Players build monuments and establishments. Monuments give special powers, and completing all monuments is the game end trigger.

It's a quick playing dice and card game, with fun fairly abstract art.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
(10/19/2014) Comment based on one play and one abandoned play.

This is a light, trivial, random, economic snowball exercise in boredom and frustration. It is perfectly suited and family-friendly to newbies and casual gamers that enjoy the randomness of Settlers, triviality of Munchkin, and card buying of Dominion, but offers little to veteran gamers and has very little replay value.

Our first game started out innocently enough, rolling a die, getting money (sometimes), and then buying buildings. Two of us ended up buying the Stadium (hits on a 6), and then proceeded to roll sixes on our turn, stealing from the third player, who was then perpetually broke all the time. We called the game right there. Strike one medium/heavy gamer off the fan list. Conclusion: lousy rolls equals lousy game. This was beginning to remind me of Kingsburg, which is another horridly random design, but the cuteness and frivolity kept me interested and willing to give it one more shot.

We played a second game to completion. One player quietly and quickly bought all of the ranches (hits on a 2), while another bought most of the forests (hits on a 5). Through great struggle, I bought most of the grocer stores (hits on a 4). In this game, I was the one who couldn't roll a "4" to save my butt. The player with the ranches then bought most of the mines (hits on an 8), before getting the second die. At that point, it was meaningless for me to get the cheese factory (hits on a 7), since I only had one ranch. From that point it was an economic snowball, and there was very little to do but wait for the sweet release of death to this boring, frustrating design. Thankfully, it only lasted 30 minutes, but this was 25 minutes too long. Started out cute, but got nasty and pointless after 10 minutes. Strike two medium/heavy gamers off the fan list.

It is an interesting, simple design, that is clean, and seemingly fun to play, with cute art and graphics. But, there are few meaningful decisions to be made, and most of it is scripted and decided by how the die falls (on every player's turn). There are limited buildings, and the point is to "cover your numbers" on the 2D6 bell curve of Settlers. You will start with a 1D6, so there is no curve at first, but a quick dash to get as many 2s first, then possibly 4s, then 5s, since these make you money on anybody's turn. Getting a farm (hits on a 1) is okay when rolling 1D6, but then is out if you start rolling 2D6, so why bother? The ranch hits on a 2 and costs the same, so this is a no-brainer. Then, if you have enough cash, start buying the 7s and 8s, which are dependent on the number of 2s and 4s that you have. The stadium hits on a 6 and steals from others, but seems pointless, since most players will be spending as much as they can. Then, get the second die, and you have covered the most probable numbers. If you hit first, then it is economic snowball from there. Rinse and repeat. It is a "6", with room to go down quickly, unless we get an expansion, because the base game's buildings don't provide enough range of strategies.

(02/21/2015) The Harbor Expansion definitely saves this borderline broken game. The larger variety of buildings, along with a completely new way of introducing them into the game, are necessary fixes. With the expansion, it is now somewhat enjoyable and fun to play. However, due to the new way where only 10 building types can come out at any given time, the game has a rather slow start. Then, there is a knee in the curve, and the game goes into overdrive, full of large swings in income, and abruptly ends. Not bad, but not great. Somewhat overstays its welcome by 20-30 minutes. The base game is now a "2", as in I'll never play it again, but with the expansion, it is a "7".

RATING: 6
Pretty good when played occasionally and briskly as a social gambling game* akin to craps; less good when played too much, like twice in a row or even the next day.  It's a big, fat, grocery store cupcake that's mostly greasy frosting; you know that while one might be okay, two or three will just make you feel gross.  So, less is more here, on all axes:  [i]session length, number of plays in a week, expectations[/i].

It's best when played as a party game with chanting and fist-pumping and exultation or slumping shame and mock table-flipping.  And lots and lots of storytelling about precisely how so-and-so bankrupted themselves by eating at every family restaurant in the tri-state area in a single, terrible evening.

[b]After 11 plays:[/b]  Better with the [thing=143789]Harbor Expansion[/thing], though you'll want to use the [thread=1315037]3 Deck/12 Pile variant[/thread] for that.

[size=7]*Not that you're risking ostracism if your number doesn't hit, and, yes, I realize there are no actual stakes—or at least not ones you're aware of while rolling those godforsaken dice.[/size]

RATING: 6
Recently received this as a gift and have played it quite a bit with the family.  The kids love this game.  The mechanics are simple enough that my 8-year old really likes it.  Unfortunately, some of the strategy is a bit over his head, so he doesn't really do very well. You really have to have a good idea of value; when is the right time to buy the second die and when should you actually roll it?  Is it better to have one card covering every possible die roll or is it better to really throw everything at just a few combinations and wait for them to trigger?

My wife and I enjoy playing it with the kids and trying to teach them some of that strategy...up until now, though, I don't think the kids have won any of the games.  They've come in second plenty of times (dice rolling helps to mitigate some of the strategy) and that is keeping them interested enough to want to try again.

For my wife and I, I could see the game getting a little stale after repeated playthroughs.  It seems like there are only a few really viable strategies and once those are mastered, it may turn into a "going through the motions"-type of experience.  I may look into the expansions to see what they add to it.

RATING: 6.8
Machi Koro is a light game with that offers players some interesting strategic choices that make it closer to gambling than any other "board game" that I've played. It can be a lot of fun when you are getting the rolls you need, and I really enjoy building up my town, but the luck can also make it somewhat frustrating.  The art is just okay in my opinion, and the cards are low quality. 

Update: The added the Harbor expansion, which really adds to the game and improves the game play and replay ability.  All plays going forward will be with the expansion.

RATING: 2
Very nice idea for a game, and the art looks great, but predictably it's just not very good. 

With the strategy in the base game being too easy given that the setup is always the same, it just comes down to luck as to who wins.

The expansion 'fixes' the game with a more cards, and changes the game so that instead of dominion style where all decks are available at the start, the expansion puts all cards into one deck and only 5? random cards are available to be purchased at any one time.

So now as well as the game being a luck fest with the dice, they've now added another layer of luck in what cards you are able to buy on your turn.

RATING: 2
I once heard someone say Machi Koro is a game built around the worst part of Catan: the luck of the dice rolls providing resources. Except now, collecting the most resources determines the winner of the game.

That's a very apt description. There really aren't any interesting decisions here. Players should just go for the building of their choice and hope the dice rolls fall their way to win.

Such simplicity might be fine for kids, but there are so many other great entry-level and filler games out there to choose from where players make decisions that actually matter.

RATING: 5
2014-11-07

Initial Rating: 6.0 (November 2014)

I wasn’t expecting much from the game, but I was pleasantly surprised. I had fun playing it. Machi Koro is a light game with lots of luck, but still fun. I feel part of the luck can be controlled so it doesn’t bother me much, however the game retains a high degree of uncontrollable luck no matter what you do. It also has a couple of interesting decisions (how many dice to roll?, what card to buy?) but they aren’t deep in any way. Reminiscent of Settlers of Catan and Dominion.

Easy to explain. The art is ok and the gameplay is good if players keep the game going at a fast pace, due to this I think it is best with 3 players, with four players there is downtime and the playtime is too long for what the game offers. Some cards are very powerful so I think there might be balance issues.

The game came in a big box with lots of wasted space (for the expansions perhaps?).
 
Bottom line, an ok family game I would play from time to time. Perhaps I will buy it in the future.

RATING: 6
I'm torn whether I like Machi Koro or not. The artwork is appealing, it's a quick playing filler game. At first, there seem to be a few different building strategies to win. But after some games, I'm more annoyed than convinced. The culprit is the production roll of a single die. The worst part from Settlers is taken while essentially most of the elements to mitigate unlucky rolls are taken away. The only way to protect against bad luck is to spread cards across all numbers which in effect will be beaten by any focused strategy with the right rolls.

RATING: 5
I enjoyed this more than I expected to. There are a few discoveries to be made regarding how buildings work together, whether by mathematics or simple observation, prompting purchases to prevent monopolies and in turn revealing other successful options, and one can imagine that after a number of such iterations you'd still have a non-trivial money engine game. Managing all the little random payouts is also less fiddly than it first appears. What holds the rating down for me personally is the fact that once potentially high-yielding investments are in place, the actual outcome can so easily and abruptly be determined by a couple of lucky rolls. The card-switching power also goes against the concept of getting to build your own slot machine.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A quick game of buying cards to build up your town to then earn more money to buy more cards.  The catch is that your engine of town cards only fruit money if you roll certain numbers on dice.  Therefore, there is some luck, but also a strategy as to how you want to focus your purchases.  Do you spread it out, so that most any roll will give you something, or do you focus a lot on certain cards, so that certain numbers will REALLY pay off?  Also, some cards will allow you to steal from an opponent if they roll a certain number on their turn, and other cards will give you money if anyone rolls that number... even outside of your turn.  Other cards give you new powers and eventually give you a win if you build the four big structures for your town.  A clever and quick game.  The 10 card variant shakes things up a bit more. On its own, I give this game an 8, but with the Harbor Expansion, it jumps to an 8.5.  Just get the expansion with the base game.

I should also note that early printings of the game have slightly longer cards than those that come with the expansions.  This makes shuffling a bit annoying when they are mixed.  This happened with my copy and I reached out to IDW and they replaced my base game cards very quickly and kindly; the cards now match the size of the Harbor Expansion cards and any future expansion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game is a bit of a disappointment.  The artwork and simplicity of play made this a much anticipated game and it would have been an automatic buy if I hadn't had the chance to play it first.  The game is susceptible to huge randomness and luck of the dice where one player can absolutely dominate by constantly getting money from the other players leaving these other players unable to accumulate any money to buy useful cards.  Such a game is frustrating and ZERO fun for the victim giving the impression that the game is broken.  Fortunately however, this is not the normal situation and given the relatively short play time, such a game would be soon over.  Usually the games are more balanced but still, the winner will likely be the one who throws his optimal die roll once or twice before anyone else.  It’s a fun and relatively fast playing game which I’ll gladly play again but probably wouldn't suggest it and don't need to own it.  Also, getting an expansion to fix the problems of the base game makes no sense.

RATING: 4
This game was WAY overhyped. Three major complaints:
1. It's all about luck. If you don't roll well, you get nothing.
2. NOT a city-building game. This is a dice-rolling, coin-collecting game whose goal is to buy four cards.
3. Red & purple cards steal coins from other players. When you only get coins due to chance/luck, it's a terrible system.
Machi Koro is ONLY for gamers who enjoy gambling!

RATING: 7.5
Machi Koro is a fast-paced game for 2-4 players. Each player wants to develop the city on his own terms in order to complete all of the landmarks under construction faster than his rivals. On his turn, each player rolls one or two dice. If the sum of the dice rolled matches the number of a building that a player owns, he gets the effect of that building; in some cases opponents will also benefit from your die (just as you can benefit from theirs). Then, with money in hand a player can build a landmark or a new building, ideally adding to the wealth of his city on future turns. The first player to construct all of his landmarks first wins!

RATING: 8
[u]Awards:[/u]
[b]Game of the Con - GenCon 2014[/b] (Shared with [gameid=147020])

[u]Comments:[/u]
From the first moment I saw an image of the Machi Koro box I knew I had fallen in love. The illustrations for this game are absolutely perfect, and the other graphic design choices just follow from those. I was lucky enough to have a friend win this in tournament at GenCon 2014 which gave me a choice to play the game a few times at the convention. At first I struggled with the dice rolling, and I started to wonder if I had made a mistake pre-ordering the game from my FLGS, but then I remembered it was a silly dice rolling game that and I should just enjoy it for what it is. After I told myself that the game really kicked into high gear and I started seeing how awesomely simple and fun Machi Koro is. I am so glad I ordered this and I can't wait to get my copy in so I can play the crap out of it. Its extremely simple to teach and so tense when playing. You pray for that big role to come up that will feed your need for buildings those wonders. When you finally do get a big roll you always feel like shouting and carrying on like a madman, and feeling like you are the smartest person in the world (which you aren't, just fyi, but that doesn't stop you from FEELING that way). I can't think of another game that is more up my alley than Machi Koro. Okay maybe if there was some shooting of lasers or negotiations it would be even more up my alley, but for what it is, it is simply awesome. Japan must be, much like Poland about five years ago, game design Valhalla.

RATING: 8
A fun, easy to learn and play card game where you buy various types of buildings/establishments in a city.  Each card has a number at the top which corresponds to the roll of a die or dice.  If you roll that number on a card, then that effect takes place.  I did notice that if you focus on buying certain cards (like the Cafe), you can gain an advantage early on (anytime anyone else rolls a '3', you get a coin).  The goal is to be the first to build your four landmarks first.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I really enjoy this game, so long as I use a house rule.  The house rule is fairly straightforward; we just play with the rules for piles that's in the expansion, except for we play with 8 piles instead of 10.  There simply aren't enough different cards in the base game to do 10 piles.

To expound: instead of starting the game with a stack of each type of card, all the cards are shuffled together in one giant stack.  Then, take cards from the top.  Every time you take a new type of card (the first Ranch, for instance), place it in a new pile.  Every time you take a card that already has a pile, just place it on that pile (the second ranch, the third ranch, etc.).  Continue until you have eight piles, then stop.

Throughout the game, whenever a pile is depleted, start drawing from the big stack again, following the same procedure.  So there's always ten piles of different cards.

If you've played the game a few times, you'll find there's basically four strategies (cheese factory, shopping mall, fruit & veggie market, and furniture factory), and because all the cards are available to be bought, there's no reason to not simply do one strategy and nothing else (barring someone else trying to do the same strategy, and depleting a necessary pile).  Once other people have started to figure out the game, it gets even less fun.

BUT, if you play with this variant, you don't have any of those troubles.  First, it's not usually possible to do strictly one strategy, because after you buy the cards available, they usually run out.  So you have to blend strategies.  Also, you don't know which cards are going to come out, so you have to weigh your risks.  You start thinking about how well a card will perform on its own if you never get the other card that combos with it.

Now granted, this variant means that there's a lot of randomness to what things are available.  Some games have a lot more red cards so there's a lot more stealing.  Some games take a while to get going if most of the piles are high numbers that no one can roll yet.  

But regardless, I still find this way much funner because a) every game has a different setup and you have to develop different strategies to deal with it, and b) the landscape of possibilities actually changes as you go through the game, opening and closing doors of opportunity.

Is it still a game of chance?  Yes.  But now there's a lot more variety instead of monotony.

Machi Koro is a short dice-rolling light strategy game.  And if you like light quick games, and you play with this variant, then you can get a lot of plays (and therefore fun) for your buck.

Oh yeah, and the components are not high quality.  They're just ok.  Especially the money cardboard uncentered tokens, bleah.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
It's really the dilution of Settlers of Catan into what the latter game REALLY is, but streamlined, substituting card drafting for area control, and better because of it (and I feel more honest of a representation of the game having no balance designed in whatsoever outside of the external factors of players feeling sorry for the disadvantaged players and ganging up on the advantaged/ahead ones).
  Roll dice to see how much money you get (like resources), build a tableau of cards that then get you more money for those rolls (like building towns/cities), keep going.  Simple family game, but doesn't have that initial two choices in the very beginning of Catan that determines one's build rate for the entire game, but rather everyone at least STARTING evenly with the same two buildings.

Will really try and sell Pop on this, saying it's just like LCR (but with actual decisions) and just as easy, and will leave it with the family who will hopefully play it a lot.
-- Failed. Just can't get the parents to play games. Sadface.

Traded away at Origins'16 Math Trade to hopefully someone that can give it more love.

RATING: 7
Machi Koro blends strategy and luck in the right way. Every time you establish a new building in your town, you're selecting your strategy. Then if you get lucky on a die roll (not necessarily your own), your strategy pays off. But your choices are not black-and-white, all-or-nothing. You get to build a different building each round, which lets your fine-tune your strategy.
The game is over quickly. I wish it lasted a bit longer.

RATING: 8
I wasn't sure if MK would be too light for me, but I was optimistic that it would work out -- and it did! This is a cute, fun little game with lots of charm and a solid amount of strategy, tactics, and interesting choices for its play length. It's also easy to teach and fun to look at.

MK reminds me of Dominion and, for some reason, Settlers. Both good things! Its balance of luck, strategy, and small footprint should keep it in the mix for some time to come. It would easily pack down to a deckbox and a small baggie for travel, too.

---

At age 7, my daughter asked to play this. She had fun with it, and asked to play it again. Her strategies aren't the best, but she's getting better -- and she has fun playing it, which is the important part.

I like it. I like it quite a bit, actually. There are certainly some familiar elements. Machi Koro feels like a blend of Dominion (communal card decks that are purchased and combined to create synergy), Atlantis Rising (building specific card-based buildings to win the game), and a bunch of other familiar dice-based games. This familiarity is not a negative, as I quite enjoy how all of these things come together to make a quality game. The dice also make the end-game exciting. You can have all the money you need to build and win on your next turn, but if someone rolls a certain number on theirs, they can steal the win away from your. FUN!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Machi Koro is a very light civilization building game.  Players start with a couple cards that represent the basic building blocks of their society.  Several piles of other cards are placed in the middle of the table which can be purchased by the players.  Every card represents a building, or place, that can be added to your society.  All these cards have a special power, usually involving getting money either from the bank, or other players.  There are still other cards - four of them per player- that they must buy to win the game.

I found there to be a few problems with Machi Koro.  First of all, turns are incredibly repetitive.  On your turn you roll one, or two dice, and take the actions that correspond to your roll, if any.  Then you buy a card if you want, and are able.  That constitutes your entire turn...every turn.  Secondly, because everything you might be able to do is dependent on a die roll the game is very luck-based.  Lastly, it looks like there's a real possibility of a runaway leader problem.

I can't recommend this game when there are so many other great games out there.  Don't waste your time on this one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5

Similar to Settlers in regard to income based on dice rolls, but offers some interesting decisions in regard to card combos, as well as different amount and possible frequency of payouts.  The game plays quickly, which makes it very approachable but also seems to prevent pursuing some deeper strategies.  Most games seem to come down to someone getting a good card combo early followed by a few lucky rolls.  Still, a unique and fun little card game if you're okay with the higher degree of randomness you would expect from a dice-centric game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In this game you are building up a tableau of buildings that can be activated either on your turn only, your opponent turns only, or on any player's turn. To activate the buildings dice must be rolled to the sum of the building in question.

The goal is to earn money in order to build each of your special buildings, which both will grant you new abilities, but also take you one step further towards winning the game, as the player who can build all of their special buildings will ultimately win the game.

It's an enjoyable game, but I have to say I much prefer Valeria: Card Kingom, which has a lot of similarities, but expands more on the idea. This is the tighter game, the more cut throat game, and you can really get screwed over by the dice rolls, either by triggering opponents ability to steal from you with their red cards, or by opponents grabbing all the blue cards before you have a chance, making their turns essentially useless for you. And I personally just prefer the smorgasboard.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Well, it's better than Dominion. Which as far as praise goes is like saying being peed on is better than being pooped on.

Oh, how the tension and excitement build as you roll a die or two, pick a card and put it in your tableau, or whatever they call it here. Probably tableau because that's a nice-sounding word that makes it seem like something elegant is going on.

And then you hope that the random numbers other people roll will be the numbers you need! Whee!

I'm not even convinced that it's actually a game. I mean, sure, it's technically a game in that it has rules and you compete against other players to win. But there isn't really a strategy (you can specialize or diversify, but either way you just hope to get lucky.) And trying not to doze off isn't really a skill.

The best thing about it is that it's short. That's all I've got, sorry.

RATING: 7
Love this game... played it 3 times with family around Christmas, and everyone had a blast.

As others have noted, this feels like a descendant of Settlers of Catan.  It scratches the same gaming itch - it is a family-weight, short length, dice driven game (heavily luck dependent).

It really lends itself to quick, somewhat strategic decisions, humerous "take that" moments, and a desire by the players to immediately play again and so that they can try a different approach.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Great fun game with very simple city-building mechanics. I like to call this game the Modern, stream-lined version of Monopoly. It has similar concepts (dice rolling on your turn, building stuff you or your opponents get "hit" on when you roll the right roll of the dice, and making enough money to eventually win the game. It also has the same "pull away" swing factor for someone who gets money, tends to make even more money and thus win the game (normal for many Eurogames as well). 

Newbies can easily pick the game up in a few turns, and figure out good strategies after just one game. The base set is a little lacking though and I wouldn't recommend this game without the Harbor Expansion.  It's far too simplistic without it. With the Harbor Expansion, rating goes up.

RATING: 7
In Machi Koro, players roll dice, and if they have a card that corresponds to the number shown on the dice, they get that card's effect. (More often than not, it's a few coins.) They can then use their coins to buy more cards, which let them get even more coins!

Machi Koro has a cute theme, and it's very easy to get into.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.3
I have only played Machi Koro once, at a local games meet-up but I rather enjoyed the game. It’s a good fun game where each player attempts to develop their town by building infrastructure (represented as card in a player’s tableau) to allow them to purchase the landmark buildings that win the game; the first player to build all the landmark buildings immediately wins the game. 

Cards are activated when their number is rolled, even if it’s not that players turn so there is the challenge for players whether to not to spread their options so they get a little something with most rolls or just to go for a ’big win’ when their lucky number comes up. Throw in cards that attack other players (usually taking either a small amount of money from everyone or a larger amount from one player) or can backfire and cause the player who holds them to lose money and there is a fair bit of fluidity to the game.

This game is well worth considering and I hear good things about the expansion.

UPDATE:

Played more games at Cancon and purchased. This one is a keeper.

RATING: 7.1
This is a card and dice game. You purchase cards which have a number on the top and activate when that number is rolled on the dice. Some cards only the person rolling can activate, others when someone else activates. The cards can stack to increase their activated power. There is a lot of interaction as you can buy cards that “attack” other players.
This game improves a lot when coupled with the Harbour expansion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Machi Koro really feels like a stripped-back Settlers of Catan in card form. This is in relation to how players gain coins each turn based on the activation number of each of their town additions (cards). 

There is some room for interesting play but when all player's are relatively matched in experience and 'gaming smarts' then the dice are going to decide the outcome in most cases. 

I can see why this one was so well received in 2014 but I also think that has a lot to do with the broadening of the hobby-base. This would not have attracted much more than a sniff of interest back in the early to mid 2000s. 

That is not a snobbish comment as I enjoy the game enough but without expansions I wouldn't want to be playing it more than 3-4 times a month otherwise it would begin to outstay its welcome. 

Good stuff for families though, the time-poor and the gateway crowd.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[edit: Yep, dropped to a 6]

I suspect that the rating of this game will drop to a 6, but for the time being it is a very pleasant game.  Easy to teach.  A vague sense that your accumulated tableau acquires a personality -- "My town is all about the mall.  My town is cranking out furniture."  This personality combined with the luck of the dice, provides a vague sense of narrative, as well.  Yes, luck is going to be the primary determinant on who wins.  If you played this game with someone who is hyper competitive, you are in for a miserable play experience.  As a family game, it's nice.

Finally, normally I don't particularly care for games with "card-conditionalities."  Keeping track of when this card does what based on these other cards.  What's nice is that Machi Kora has this, but only to a very, very modest degree.  Further, the conditionalities are thematically supported.  The furniture factory and forests go together.  The cheese factory and the dairies go together.  Etc.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
During their turn, players roll 1 or two dice. They then use coins to purchase buildings. Some buildings allow you to get coins during your turn, some earn coins during other people's turns, or some allow you to take coins from other people. The first player to build all 4 landmarks wins the game. 

This game has been compared to a lighter version of Settlers of Catan. It's a fun filler, and you can choose what strategy to pursue. You do need some luck for your buildings to come up. Some people think there is too much luck. I'm certainly looking forward to more plays of this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Machi Koro epitomizes the use of dice I despise in games: Plan first, then roll to see if you succeed. A good roll and things work, a bad roll and you've wasted a turn.

Granted Machi Koro's purchases allow a player to try and mitigate bad dice rolls by improving the odds of success, but its still a chance game. If you consistently roll poorly, which is a statistical possibility, all the planning in the world won't save you.

The more I played, the more I began to realize I should have taken Machi Koro's childish art design as a clue to how simplistic and bereft of depth the game would prove to be.

RATING: 8

RATING: 7.35
I had been following this game for a long time. I was planning to do a print and play version, until I heard was going to publish it in Spanish. Then I decided to wait... and they have just published it, and we got to try it :-)

The rules are super-easy to learn. It's everything in each card, but even if the cards have text, you don't need time to understand then, etc. The setup is also fast. Even if you're a noob, in 5 minutes you can be playing.

It is a fun dice luck filler with a speedy gameflow; even if the luck is definitly the factor, you can make nice combos and do some strategy with the buildings you choose. Then it can work or not, depending your luck with the dice.

The game will finish fast enough for you not to annoy if Lady Luck does not smile upon you.

I'm quite geeky with dice games and I was worried the cards could not be balanced (in fact, they can't be balanced since some of them work on your rivals turn, and they will be more or less useful depending on the number of players). Also, there are some cards better than others, but everything is quite balanced.

Maybe its replayability will suffer on the long run, because even if there is no small number of different cards, their count is not very high either.

Overall, a very fun dice filler :-)

PS: I was also concerned about the snowball effect: for the moment, I didn't see any worth mentioning.
