RATING: 8
Money falling from the sky Catan-style, distilled to the bare minimum. No way of catching up, so this has to be played fast to be worth the time spent.

Might not have that much replayability without randomizing the card market but  that can be easily done, especially with the expansions. Also, the rulebook in the Kosmos edition already includes a variant for randomization. 

Very simple and instantly recognizable card effects, so buying the tiny Kosmos edition is not a problem at all. In addition to being in a very conveniently sized box, the Kosmos edition is printed in Germany and also has an extra icon telling whether a card works on everyone's turn or your turn only, so even the need to memorize the card color has been removed. 

Still, I find it a bit silly to have any text on the cards at all, since the effects could be very easily communicated through simple and large enough icons, making the cards even more easily recognizable from the other side of the table. Of course, the expansion cards might have too complex effects for this to work.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This city building game provides for a light and enjoyable 20 minute game. On the weekend we opened it, Machi Koro effortlessly took the top spot and was played at least a dozen times a day with different players rotating in and out!

Players take turns throwing a die, collecting money from the buildings they have, sometimes collecting money on other players turns (blue cards) and then buying one new building on that turn. The goal of the game is to flip over and construct the Train Station, Shopping Mall, Amusement Park, and the Radio Tower.

This is a fast game and easily understood. I have played it with the whole family from a six year old to sixty year old. It’s one of those games where most players are able to look at what they accomplished at the end of the game and be happy even if they didn’t win. Then they want to play again.

Each player only starts with a wheat field and a bakery. Players throw one die and the result may give them more income on their turn or on other players turns. You then buy new cards that will expand when and how you get money. You will even be able to steal from other players. Eventually, you can throw two dice and access the more generous and powerful cards that activate above 6.

This game does have some runaway mechanics in it that rely on luck, especially in a 2 player game. Once a player builds multiples of one card or gets certain powers, they are much more likely to compound that benefit for a victory. This is countered in games with more players by the fact that there are only so many of each card and that the purple and red cards allow others to steal from the player that is furthest ahead without making it a Catan-like highway of frustration for the leading player.

RATING: 7
I agree with those who say it's a simple filler and those expecting it to be some kind of deep strategy game are not evaluating the game on its merits. However, to play it with non-gamers or as a simple, pleasant filler or warm-up game, it fills those niches. 

The rules are underwritten in that aspects of various rules aren't fully explained. Although the rule book is short, it could have used less artwork and a smaller, tighter font, with fuller text and more examples of play. This is evidenced by the extensive listing of rules questions on BGG, despite the game's relative simplicity. Once I wrote out my own version the game wasn't hard to understand. 

Purchased February 2015. Subsequently purchased the Harbor Expansion after reading many comments about how it improves the basic design. Would like to get non-gamers or light-game gamers into this one to see how well they like it, as well as to gauge how it plays with several people.

RATING: 7
Simple concept but well executed. I've only played the base set with the expansion drafting rules (10 available card types at any one point). Buy cards into your tableau, roll dice hoping to activate your cards Roma-style. Each card type has a different way to earn money, be it you only, on anyone's turn, steal from other players, etc. So there's choices on whether to spread across the numbers or invest in a few numbers and aim for jackpot turns. There's combos you can shoot for. Every turn buy another card to add to your tableau (or save up for a better turn, but risk losing your money to other players in the meantime), so turns become more consistent and "bigger" as the game goes on. All good stuff. Turns are fast, there's plenty of cheer factor in the die rolling, and you can always blame the dice when you lose. Which will probably be true anyway, but it doesn't pretend to be anything but a meaty filler anyway. Happy to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
"Money Engine - The Card Game".

A Blast ! Very Cute Art !  City building card game with no hand management. Easy to learn, play, and teach. I love how all the information is open, and all buildings are available (until they're gone). Fun as 2-player, but better with more. My gaming wife kicks my butt at this one.

Potentially contains the same dice mechanism (and thus downtime) as Catan, except you may roll a single die if players choose to on their turns. However, games do move along well enough.

Though I love this vanilla, I think the expansion makes it better.

RATING: 6
It's fun, fast and light. It's been a great success with some of my family, include my young nieces. It's cute and it works well for what it is. But ultimately, it's been a disappointment. It's too light to hold my interest and really there aren't that many options for you to go for and everybody is doing basically the same thing. Also,  it really doesn't work as well for two players - it makes green cards much stronger and red cards weaker and it limits even further the options the game provides.

The whole point of a good gateway game is that your family can enjoy it casually while you, the gamer, can enjoy the strategizing that's mitigated by the dice. Machi Koro succeeds in the first and fails in the second, as it really doesn't give you much to play with in terms of strategy.

I'm looking forward to trying it with the expansion, which I heard fixes some of these issues and will make it into a game that I can enjoy while playing with people who are more casual. However, any game that _requires_ the expansion for you to have fun with it - is flawed in my book. This is a shame, because it really is a cute and attractive theme that's well produced. 

Also, the money tokens are pretty bad and the box is really huge for no good reason. True, there might be lots of expansions but even with the one that'll be shortly out, this box would be much too big. I would have preferred to have the bigger box come with one of the later expansions.

RATING: 6
Machi Koro is a quick dice-based filler.  You buy cards from a Dominion-like market that tells you what you get on various die rolls--dice rolls here affect all players like Settlers of Catan.  While enjoyable, I found the game to be lacking in depth and variety.  There's only one resource (money), and the cards that you can buy are fixed, so you're presented with the same choices in each game.  Additionally, some of the cards featured take-that abilities that I didn't care for.  The system has potential if the game had a Dominion-scale assortment of cards to choose from, but as it stands, it's somewhat bland.

RATING: 6
This game was quite delightful. It draws comparisons with Settlers of Catan, and I think the comparison is justified. The thing that soured me on Settlers was how trading dragged the game to a halt. I think this was the result of the group I played with more than anything, but nevertheless it made for negative play experiences. Machi Koro distills the random resource production and engine building of SoC down to a 20-30 minute light game. I could see pulling this out with almost any group, and I am looking forward to the US release.

RATING: 4
This game is a very strange combination of deterministic gameplay and sheer randomness. When played according to the base game rules, each player essentially follows the same path with very little strategy involved. The only difference between players is the luck of the dice. The winner tends to be the person who happens have the lucky roles needed to repeatedly steal all of their opponent's money. You win by ensuring your opponent can't really do anything on their turn. The game plays better with 3 or 4 players than with 2. Note that the game is improved somewhat by the Harbor Expansion.

RATING: N/A
A delightful game of economic competition.  I highly recommend that even if you don't own the Harbor Expansion, you play with its "marketplace" rule anyway.  I also recommend splitting the deck into properties with die value 1-6 and properties of 7-12 and dealing each of them to its own five-slot marketplace, to avoid the early game gumming up on overly expensive properties.

(Also, if your playgroup includes younger children, you should remove the Business Centers before play.  Exchanging your Wheat Field for someone's Mine or Forest is just so oppressively harsh it can rob the game of its light-hearted nature if all players can't take such a blow with aplomb.)

RATING: N/A
P: 2-4, T: 30 min, SdJ-N
City building, Dice fueled engine builder. "Catan-like/light" dice rolling to determine which resources (buildings) trigger; OR Monopoly-like dice rolling to trigger opponents buildings.  Varied ways to build your engine. Fairly light and quick playtime.
-Unlikely dice rolls can may play a prominent roll. Not regarded as well with only 2p, need more interactions. Some fairly cutthroat ways to approach the engine building.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This is a light game where you roll a die and then resolve the effects of cards in front of you that match that die face. As you play the game, you buy new cards, so you can diversify your numbers to get effects more often, or you can stack copies of the same card (with a few minor exceptions) and hope for the best. In our game, I diversified, bought a second die to roll (so I had cards for 1-12) and wound up 1 turn behind the other player who stuck to only numbers 1-6. It's a cute game but it has a real lack of re-playability. I wanted expansions to help that but the expansions are so poorly designed that it honestly ruined the game for me.

:star: Art and components
:nostar: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:nostar::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I love the artwork of this Japanese marble. The game play is so lovely easy. A player rolls a die and then look up your cards if one of them generates you income. Some cards generate income at any person's turn, some at your turn only or a few even only when somebody else rolls.
Income is in coins and at your turn you may purchase a building. You can pick any building from the middle of the table or you may build one of the four own unfinished buildings where you started with. The first player to complete there four buildings wins Machi Koro!
Great lovely artwork, nice, easy and engaging game play. Awesome!

RATING: 8.11
Extremely simple game.  It's hard not to fall in love with Machi Koro on your first play.....but you may get bored relatively quickly.  Few combos are hard to ignore (buy ranches/cheese factories or buy forests/mines/furniture factories...then roll 7s and 8s).

I haven't yet tried the Harbor expansion, but hope it will add replay-ability (via multiple paths to victory).

I see this as a great gateway game for people who enjoy rolling-dice.  Less barrier to entry than Catan (and less time to complete the game), but you still feel the power of building an engine that will launch you to victory.

Whether or not you have the expansion, I would strongly recommend using the 10 cards available at any time (rather than having all the cards available) variant.

Recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.8
this game is a streamlined game or card drafting with dice rolling.  At first there doesn't seem to be much with the game as it starts a little slow but interesting decisions begin to develop as you decide whether to roll one or two dice and which cards to buy.  There can be a defensive aspect to this thought process which is where the puzzle aspect begins.  Now it's probably not a game to take too seriously because you are working with probabilities with a die or two but entertains non-the -less.  Probably needs an expansion or two as you use every card in the base game each game which will get old quickly.   The Harbor expansion rectifies the clunky start and changes the set-up to create more variety.  Make this original game seem complete.

I would definitely recommend the Millionaire's Row and Harbour expansions, as these add a lot of replayability (if a little too much randomness for some tastes).

RATING: 5.8
this game kills Monopoly for most people i've met (although i chalk this up to demand for good games among muggles going unmet for so many years and familiarity with "roll two dice, get money, build a house"). we very much enjoyed the base game, but it got very stale ("the twos are gone so it's time to see who'll be first to roll a four and buy a seven"). the Harbor expansion's new market layout was quite refreshing, but would often really swing the game. even using the Bright Lights modified layout with Millionaire's Row cards wasn't really enough to bring back the magic. my number of logged plays probably has more to do with this being a quick, accessible, replayable game rather than it being good. Card Kingdoms Valeria beats this game hands down for gamers' light engine building filler. Machi Koro still wins out the "family theme" battle, but i'm not sure i care to keep it around another few years just in case my in-laws need a non-Monopoly gateway dice chucker.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Simple fast light random fun. Reminds me a bit of The City (you gradually add more and more powerful cards to your city).

It seems to risk "runaway leader" syndrome as the rich get richer, and it can take longer than I'd expect to get enough money to finish, as it can be hard to save money over several turns for the high cost cards, due to lots cards which let someone else steal some money from you. But despite this, I still found myself wanting to play "just one more" because it's fun gradually building up your city. :)

Uses a lot of table space if you want to keep each of your types of collected cards visible and separate in a row. So far I played only 2-player and our fairly large table was very crowded and we kept having to rearrange our ever-growing city...

RATING: 3
27-03-2015. Rerated from 4 to 3 after playing Harbor expansion. Wow. I dislike Machi Koro even more with Harbor than without. 26-01-2015. Rated after 4 plays. Machi-Koro is the hotness.. or was it a short while back.

Machi Koro is an extremely easy game in which you build a city. Each round you roll a die (or two dice depending on an upgrade) and see if you get resources. All buildings have a value on the top that, if that number is rolled by the player, it triggers. Some buildings give you as active player money, some buildings give all players money and for some cards you have to pay when you roll it.
Whomever buys their 4th town upgrade first wins. 

The game is ok. I liked it as a very short filler. It's ok, it's decent.. but there is not much here. This game is INCREDIBLY light.

RATING: 8
**NOTE:  People I know who own this game said that several modifications and adaptations to the rules/setup were needed to balance out the game play and flow of the game in order to make it reasonably playable.  My review is based on using those mods.

Pros:
- Easy to learn and play
- Strategy with luck
- Nice artwork and theme
- Fast setup
- No wait times -- some cards are in effect on everyone's turn

Cons:
- The luck part can be pretty inconvenient.  You may be unlucky enough to never have any of the cards you need available on your turn for purchase.  You may also be unlucky enough to not have your numbers rolled very often though that can be compensated for by making sure you have diverse cards (assuming that you are able to buy them on your turn, a catch-22 thing).
- If you have a slow start, you fall behind, or if you're unlucky, it can be really hard (if not impossible) to catch up to the lead player, especially if you do not have any of the red cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Very enjoyable light game, with familiar elements from games that I know and love. It has the Settlers element, of start your turn by rolling dice and see what players get. It has the Dominion element of a market of cards available for purchase, and the cards, if added to your "deck" give you additional powers. It has the Splendor/Saint Petersburg element of engine-building, with the strategy of timing your transition from the engine-building to the VP chase. All in all, a very solid gateway game that plays about 10 minutes/player, and plays very well (while differently!) with 2, 3, or 4 players. I have not tried the expansion yet.

UPDATE: I've now tried the game with the variable set-up, and expansion. I quite like the variable set-up. I found the expansions, while interesting, made the game drag on a bit. So a trade-off there.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
This is sort of like Settlers of Catan with the following changes:
 - Board and spatial elements removed
 - Resource cards replaced with coins
 - Basic building tree replaced with a "market" of cards with more interesting abilities

So, it's not all that similar to Catan, but it keeps the core of rolling dice each turn and triggering things for everyone, not just the current player, which I think is a major draw to Catan.  I would most often choose this over Catan, even though I value lower luck and do enjoy spatial elements to games.  I enjoy the control I have in choosing which cards to buy from the varying market, and the satisfaction of having these chosen purchased cards activate on the dice rolls is just a lot more satisfying to me than the resource gaining from terrain in Catan.  

The game does have its flaws; I'm not sure the cards are particularly balanced, but this is somewhat mitigated by the fact that all cards are up for grabs by all players... mostly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The more I play this game, the more I like it. It's so simple. Just roll the dice, earn money, and buy more cards. Finding cards that work together, and then getting the die roll that makes them pay out is what the game's all about. It's super fun. I see my rating rising with repeated play.

RATING: 4
Light and silly, but the sudden end just seems a bit anticlimactic. Choose from a raft of buildings and hope to roll the right numbers a lot. I'd prefer a Roll for the Galaxy type finish where the fourth landmark purchase triggers the end and then players count up the worth of their buildings (perhaps a 1-3 VP rating for each building).

If you're looking for a light family game dumbed down from Settlers of Catan then this is it. Trading is gone. Resources types have been consolidated into one - money. The race for position on the board is now just a grab for certain cards. It's a decent base system that could easily have been jazzed up a bit without adding much complication. Instead it's just too simple for me.

Really needs the harbour expansion to take it up another notch.

RATING: 3
This game just... doesn't work for me. It's so simple my fiance and I quit halfway through because it was boring for both of us, and funny thing she's the casual gamer not me.

Mechanically this game is a money-collecting dice-roller, plain and simple. I wouldn't accept an argument about it being anything other than that. You roll a dice, collect money, buy things to get you more money, get enough money you buy things that let you win. That's it. Snore.

The components are actually really nicely packaged, the money pieces are really nice, and the cards have absolutely amazing art. 

This game meets a certain type of audience and setting and that just isn't mine. Ridiculously Casual, Rules so simple kids could play. To a lot, that's an amazing plus and I would play this again but only WITH kids and only if a huge collective of my gaming group has some undying thirst to play.

As it stands I only have the vanilla so I can't say how much improvement the expansion will make (I will never purchase the expansion unless it's less than $5USD). 

Sorry Masao Suganuma, you just don't appeal to me with this game.

RATING: 7.8
Machi Koro --

Played three-player.

Simple mechanics and interesting interplay.  In the basic game, I found myself selecting which cards to purchase based on the dice value which would activate the card and on the number of cards remaining of that type.  When playing with the expansion, the cards available were so random that I could not afford to delay the purchase of a card for fear that another of that type might not become available soon.

I really liked both versions of the game.  The basic version is more elegant, but the expanded version introduces more interesting interactions.

In both cases, the game starts a bit slowly, but as the game progresses, each player builds momentum.  I don't like the snowball effect when it is limited to the leader, but in this game, everyone will develop their own momentum.  Fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Machi Koro is a cheerfully illustrated fusion of [thing=13][/thing]'s dice activation system and [thing=36218][/thing]'s customisable combos. Players build a tableau by buying establishments, and race to construct all 4 personal landmarks before anybody else. 

The game's charm lies in creating combos and the interaction which keeps players involved throughout. However, there aren't sufficient incentives for players to roll two dice as opposed to just one. This is a major issue, because it effectively halves whatever strategic depth that the game offers. [i](Disclaimer: I haven't tried the expansion variant in which the set-up is randomised.)[/i]

There are other minor problems which are easier to rationalise away. Luck can snowball to crown a runaway winner or freeze a player out, but that's acceptable for a light dice game. The game also has a tendency to drag with 4 players. In short, Machi Koro is fun to explore for a few games, after which its static set-up and lack of card variety drags its appeal down sharply. Its expansions do address the base game's more glaring issues though.

RATING: 2.5
So I really wanted Machi Koro to be my Catan/Dominion hybrid that would be the best of both worlds, but alas it was not. Machi Koro has a major problem that we ran into in the first game and the problem has been in every game since. Sticking to one die ALWAYS beats out rolling two dice. So the second one person realizes this the game becomes a rush to buy all the low level buildings which is really repetitive and not much fun. The art in the game is great and the idea is great I just feel like a little more playtesting could have really helped this game out. I'll hold onto it for now and hope that a future expansion fixes some of the games glaring issues, but until then I will not be touching this game again.

RATING: 2
Machi Koro recipe:

Step 1: Start with one part Dominion

Step 2: Mix in two parts Settlers of Catan

Step 3: Remove the fun parts of both games. Done!

You buy cards to... get more money to buy more cards with. Sound like Dominion? Yeah, except there aren't any cool combos and you never really feel "powerful"

You also roll the die on every turn to see who's stuff activates. Sound like Settlers? Yeah, but you won't be doing anything fun like trading or blocking people or interacting really at all.

Rolling the die and waiting for stuff to happen just isn't fun.

(1 Design + 1 Enjoyment)

The game is essentially gamer craps with a customizable payout scheme.  It's fun, but a little too high variance for my personal preference.  I think there are probably a few broken strategies with the base set, but the expansions certainly help with this.

RATING: 9
Surprisingly good and pretty elegant. Essentially, you are making bets on which numbers get rolled through your purchases. There can be a bit of a runaway leader issue if someone gets a couple of lucky rolls and can prevent other players from amassing much of a bankroll, but fortunately, the game is pretty short, so it's not that bad. I think what really draws me in is racing to get some buildings built that give you money on common rolls and then seeing those bets pay off. It does a nice job of keeping everyone involved, as some of your buildings pay on anyone's turn.

UPDATE: The Harbor expansion really balances things out quite nicely, as it adds some wronky cards in the middle ranges, as well as some interesting options where you can turn 10+ into 12+.

RATING: 6
1 play, 4er

The game feels a bit like a light version of Settlers with special powers: you roll 1-2 dice, collect stuff (money), and use it to buy other stuff. And you generally want to spend what you got, so other people don't steal it from you! Going through the rules, it seemed like it might be fun. The early game, however, was disappointingly uninteresting: everyone pretty much acquired the same cards and had basically the same tableau. 

I'd like to try it again -- it's quick enough, at least -- but not sure that this would be a keeper.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
Each player starts with 2 face-up cards and 4 face-down objective cards. The display has several copies of 15 or so cards. Each card is color-coded and numbered (1-12): the number(s) tell you when that card activates, and the colors tell you if they activate only on your own turn (green), on other players' turns (red), both (blue); or if they have a special power (purple). 

On your turn, roll 1 or 2 dice (your choice, once you've built one of your 4 objective cards). Cards with that number (or sum) may activate, earning their owner money or taking money from other players. Players may earn coins based on the roll. Then, you may purchase a card from the Display, or flip one of your 4 objective cards (the first player to buy all 4 wins the game).

RATING: 4
Yawn.  Imagine taking one of the mechanics of Settlers of Catan, rolling dice for resources, and stripping almost everything else out of it.

There's nothing inherently wrong with this game, it's just a bit boring because there's not much there.  Each turn a player rolls a die.  To start a roll of 1 gets everyone a coin and a roll of 2 or 3 on your turn gets only you a coin.  With this money you buy new cards that give you money based on other rolls.  Some reward whenever anybody rolls a number, some only when you roll it, and there are even two kinds of attack cards that steal money when either they or you roll a number.

The goal is to save up enough money to build four structures.  Each of these structures also gives you a power with the cheapest one allowing you to roll two dice opening up the options for which numbers are rolled and which buildings you should purchase.

There is only the smallest amount of strategy in which buildings to buy.  Obviously when you unlock two dice the numbers with the highest probability (7, 6 and 8, etc.) are the most worthwhile to pay attention to but other than that this is really just a game of luckiest roller wins.

RATING: 6.5
The base game by itself presents a case of great potential and clever mechanics faulted by the presence of a dominant strategy - the well-known Cheese & Factory as well as Convenience Store strategies - that are probabilistically much more viable. While this can be mitigated by all players going for the ingredients for these items equally, that merely moves the 'equilibrium' to another backup strategy, basically rendering the game 'solvable'. Future expansions are expected to fix this problem especially with the updated rule of only displaying a given number of unique establishments available for purchase at a time, but the base game as it is resolves to little more than mindless dice-rolling that rarely proceeds away from a 1-dice strategy, rendering half of the cards useless and the excitement of figuring out a tactic or strategy nearly non-existent as players can keep perusing any one or two build orders they devised in their first couple of plays.

RATING: 6
[Go Kids 16 edition] {City; dice-rolling, card-drafting; optimal 4p}  I got this game as a gift from a friend.

Machi Koro is a little dice game. I don't think it's a filler because the setup and the game play is not really fast-paced. In this game, players try to build their cities and buildings.  Each turn, the active player roll one or two dice and then players may collect income.  Afterwards, each player may buy one buildings.

Player interaction is fair because players can collect income in other players' turn or steal money from others.  A typical game can be fun and engaging, but I don't think replayability is particularly good.

The strategy is simple; try to maximise your income in a single roll.  I don't think spreading the buildings across different numbers is wise.

It is easy to teach the game.  My daughters can understand the game immediately.

The game component is so so.  The card illustration is cute, but not attractive.  The box is absolutely larger than necessary... BAD!

RATING: 5

RATING: 6
Machi Koro is a good introductory economic game - what could be more simple? Buy small buildings to make some money, use that money to buy bigger buildings which make more money, first to hit a certain amount wins the game. There's dice rolling too, which levels the playing field between skilled and less skilled players, and adds a certain level of obfuscation as to the best strategies. 

Unfortunately, the long-term prospects of Machi Koro aren't so good. It becomes clear after a few plays that certain strategies are better than others, and any solutions to this problem (as in the expansions) simply add larger amounts of luck to the game to try to hide this fact. 

In the final analysis the nitty-gritty balance of Machi Koro is not well designed. After maybe five plays I was done with it. Out of my collection it goes, which really is unfortunate - it could have been THE go-to gateway economic game. It could have been a contender - but, no.

RATING: 7
While it has the buzz, I would really not recommend to play this with 5 players.. Game was drawn out to a stunning 3 hours, which is about 2 hours too long for what it offers. Roll, recalculate, (hopefully buy), pass the die. Rinse and repeat, rinse and repeat. Awful.

As a result of my 5p experience, I don't see this game having a lot of staying power once the buzz wanes.

[Edit aug 2016] we played this again with 4p and it was an enjoyable experience. I realised my bad 5p experience was with the expansion using rules as written. Now, however, we played base only with 10 slots open variant. Will never play otherwise again. It transformed this game from a dull dominion type ripoff into a enjoyable accessible game. In fact, my kids were so interested that i bought a copy myself and we have enjoyed quite some pleasant afternoons playing this game. Solid family game IF: play no more than 4 AND use 10 slots open variant.
Rating revised upwards by one point.

RATING: 6.8
This game caused quite the hype when it first came out, and now it's finally available to a wider audience, which is why I finally got to play it.
The goal is to build 4 cards before anyone else does. To build them you need money. To get money you buy cheaper cards that give you money. So all you do in this game is spend money with the hopes of getting more money. But there seems to be only one strategy: get cards so you get money on as many die results as possible. Then double up for some combos and hope for that roll that gives you a lotta mulas.
I'm not sure how much depth is actually here but it sure is enjoyable to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
For a game so charmingly illustrated, it's a yawn. How sad. Even with the Harbor expansion, which seems absolutely necessary, I found myself wondering where the interesting decisions were hiding. There are certainly decisions to be made, but the game does its best to make everyone feel like they are accomplishing something each turn even if, in the end, you're merely building up a tableau of more powerful cards. It's a snowball of a game, where momentum is everything. The dice add a touch of randomness, and you'll need to spread out your buildings such that you cover as many numbers as possible (thus ensuring you collect something each turn), but the probabilities are hardly challenging to anticipate. For a game that is about racing to build your prestige buildings before anyone else, there was no tension until the very end -- and then the game was over. Where's the race? Where's the jockeying for cards and position?

In the city-building genre, I'd much prefer the new Quadopolis for something at the charming introductory level that is about optimizing points, or Suburbia for something at the advanced level that is about card/tile interactions. Neither game captures everything Machi Koro is about, but they each stand apart as better games. And that's the disappointing thing about Machi Koro. What it attempts to combine is interesting, but it never manages to rise about the level of interesting experiment.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I played this game once with 4 players and saw no hope for the game. This is strictly a dice rolling game. It doesn't matter how good your strategy is, unlucky dice rolling will doom you. The strategy feels like it will be the same thing in every game. There was way too much "I rolled and received nothing. My turn is over." You know how that happens in early-mid game Settlers and then you have to wait for a couple of minutes while your opponents successfully roll their number and have to make decisions? Yeah, same thing here.

This isn't the type of game that I enjoy. Elder Sign is the game that I immediately thought of and compared this game to. I strongly disliked Elder Sign at first, but much like eating a food you don't enjoy, the more I did it, the more tolerable it became. I can now tolerate Elder Sign (though I don't enjoy it). That's what this game feels like - I hate it at first, but if I play it a couple of dozen times, it can become tolerable. 

This game is all about dice rolling. Sit around and watch everyone roll dice! Woo!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Settlers of Catan is brilliant in it's mix of easy to learn, deep to master; complex enough to satisfy hardcore gamers, approachable enough to involve your family members. Another good thing about it is it's a mix of strategy and randomness... Odd tile or number placement could result in some very strange and uneven turns, leading to lead upsets and keeping things interesting. While that part of the randomness is nice, on the other hand, being pigeon-holed into having resources denied to you is an easy way lose for sure, and really frustrates.
Machi Koro has all the same virtues, some randomness, plenty of strategy, fun for hardcores and casuals, dead simple to explain and learn, constant room to vary your strategies. It takes all that, and improves it... remarkably. 
First off, you always have access to all buildable structures. The only resource is money, and you use to build everything/anything. As a result, you have CONSTANT control over what your strategy is... no having to make a plan B because you can't get access to Stone or whatever.  You chose to rely on certain numbers or diversify. There's a random element, but it's basically all on you... Rarely did I feel cheated by the dice.
Also cool is no tiles... I don't know if some people LIKE tiles, but to me, they're a necessary evil... a randomized terrain is a super cool feature, tile decks are a way to make it happen, so you put up with them... but lord, what an awful way to simulate it... nudge anything and the whole board is a mess, the play field keeps trying to wander into player spaces or off your table, and it's a pain to put down and clean up. Machi Koro ditches all of that by reducing it's entire game to a set of cards, a pair of dice, and some money tokens... and a piece of scratch paper or a notepad program on a smartphone lets you ditch the token. As a result you can carry this game in your standary deck box. Love it. I can (and do) bring it everywhere, I always have a board game on me. Freaking brilliant.
If you're a real hardcore nerd type that likes everything better if it involves some elves and magic swords, you may want to consider looking at Card Kingdoms Valeria instead (as a slightly more deep/fun but also dorkier title) if your purse strings are tight... personally I think a good board game collection could make room for both though.
My one note? Think carefully about expansions. Like Settlers, which it bares so much resemblance to, I advise against the expansion packs to date... It's true they add a few nice new cards, most notably the expanded win condition cards, dragging out game length (which I like... again this game is pleasantly fast, sometimes it feels that just as things were getting truly exciting, someone is in striking distance of the win inside a turn), but they aren't terribly necessary and alter some of my favorite aspects of play for the worst.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
2-5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
The amount of luck is huge. Sure part of the game is deciding whether to spread out on numbers 1-6, or start rolling two dice and go for 6-8, but still the person who has better luck will always win.

Also, at least in the base game, there seems to be a dominant strategy. You buy as many of the number 5 cards as you can, then eventually you buy the card that gives you extra money for having the 5 cards (I think it's Furniture Factory). This has won every game I've played, and it just comes down to who rolls the dice they need to trigger that combo the fastest. I've gotten over $50 on one roll with that strategy, which is enough to win the game, and there's really no counter to it. I'm hoping the expansion fixes this issue because I really want to be able to approach this game with different strategies.

Update: The expansion somehow made the game worse. I thought the variety would help, but I didn't consider a couple of things: luck can be an even bigger issue because now if one of the really good/overpowered cards comes out then whoever gets it is at an advantage. The game is also even longer because a lot of the buildings just don't synergize very well so you end up just collecting a few dollars at a time, hoping for a good building to come up.

It seems that there is just no way to make this game fun, despite the premise sounding fun.

Sold.

RATING: 4
Machi Koro is a very light dice rolling game. You roll some dice and score some coins, which you use to buy buildings... which help you score coins on dice rolls. And eventually someone gets enough coins to build all their "special" buildings. And that wins the game.

So yeah... there's some strategy here... but it's mostly just luck of the roll. Even for a short game, this was a little too random for me. It's not horrible, but there's not much that made me want to repeat the experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Machi Koro has a limited number of solid strategies for buying establishments, which becomes evident after just a play or two. Once a player decides on their strategy, they'll simply buy the establishments that fit in with that strategy as they can, and then hope the dice rolls favor their numbers more than their opponents'.

The synergy of some establishments is so strong that a lucky roll or two can supply enough coins to buy all the goal establishments and win the game. I would prefer if the 'right' answers weren't so extremely right, I guess.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Together with Blueprints, the best filler dice game of Essen 2013 IMO. In this game, you build a small city with building cards, in order to obtain more and more money on a certain dice roll. There are multiple buildings of each type in the game, and they sometimes combo with other buildings, so the game offers decisions akin to any deckbuilder: Shall I take another one of those cards before the pile runs out? Or is this the right time to go for the victory cards? And all of that in a 30 - 40 minute game. Magic.
UPDATE: After playing the game many times more with two, I am looking forward to the expansion. Without it, there seems to be an optimal strategy (or two), and the game is getting a little same-ish.
UPDATE: With the Harbor expansion, I am rating this game an 8.5! Great stuff!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I am breaking with tradition. This is one game where an expansion (Machi Koro Plus) is a must for an enjoyable game. The expansion introduces a new method of playing using a random pile of 10 buildings available for purchase each round that is constantly being replenished. This randomization is key in making the game more fun and variable, not to mention more strategic. The static, basic core setup gets stale extremely fast as the opening moves for each game seem limited. Using the randomized setup, players must adapt with what is available on the onset and also react to how other players are setting up their economy engine. Fun! The ratings with the core set is a 4 while with the expansion, the game becomes a 7. 

I rarely if ever endorse an expansion. This is a rare exception and one that is crucial for a gamer to enjoy the game.

RATING: 6
A fun, light game that's fast to learn.

Unfortunately, the base game has a huge flaw in that it's very easily mathematically "solved" (you aren't guaranteed to win as it's still random, but the best play is clear). This isn't a complex affair, either -- it was evident in the first few games of playing it, and after some googling to make sure I wasn't crazy, I found people statistically confirming it with play simulations and everything.

So, you need house rules to solve that. I never really tried any and the game ended up getting shelved after 5 or so plays until we got expansions. Still, it was a fairly enjoyable session and a cheap game, and house rules wouldn't be terribly difficult to implement.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Machi Koro is a very good game for occasional players. For more hardcore gamers, the base game is "limited". Let's explain it.

Let's say the game is in part hasardous because of the dice. Ok, it's not a problem when you know it and accept it. The winner can be chosen by a dice, sometimes ...

The problem is that it is an equilibrium game. You should never let a player empower in a strategy (the cafe/restaurant strategy or the baker strategy or ...).

So playing with people that doesn't know the game as well as you became boring ... cause they let you make your big combos.

BUT ... with extension, you break everything, because you can't choose your card as freely than in base game. So, use the extension ;-)

RATING: 6

"But," I hear you saying, "you should have bought one of each possible die roll!"  Yes, but when I rolled three for three turns straight and had to give my money to all the folks with the Cafe, it was pretty damned annoying.  I like the way you build up an engine, and the way the game entices you to spend all your money each turn, but I'm not fond of the aggressive cards.

UPDATE: The game improves by adding Plus (the stacks of cards is variable each game, preventing a lot of scripted beginnings to the game), and also by playing with fewer players.  In fact, this might be a best-with-two game.

2 plays: 2 players
3 play: 4 players

RATING: 5
Macho Koro is a great game. It is sort of a modern day version of Catan without the board. The game revolves building a tableau of cards which collect coins based of the roll of the dice, and the first to complete a collection of 4 landmark buildings wins. The game is easy to teach and fast to play. There is a degree of luck involved, but there is also strategy involved in the choices of which buildings you buy in what order and it is a lot of fun.

RATING: N/A
Dice and card drafting city builder with some interactive screwage. Quick fun. I like how the game plays. Roll a die, get paid, buy cards to better your odds to make more money to build your engine to buy the game winners. So simple but how much do you value the card price with the payoff?  Captures some of the excitement of gambling without the loss! The great thing is the Catan aspect where every player roll can impact several player thus holding everyone's interest. And, it is addictive buying cards for your city. Buy general cards that hit on every player roll or just your own, or cause them to give you points--love that decision and play interaction.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I can not quite understand why people compare this game to Catan. It is also not a real city-building game. It is just a simple dice-rolling game about building up a set of cards that let you acquire money and have some special features. So the important thing is to buy cards that fit your strategy and that work well in combination with each other. 

What I like about this game is that you can use cards from the base game or from one of the expansions just the way you want to. If you dislike a special card, just do not use it. Balancing is not affected in every way, it just makes the game longer. This also opens up the game for custom or special cards. If the players know how every card works the standard rule where every card is available anytime may be quite interesting and the game gets very fast-paced, but may also become a bit boring after some plays when everyone uses the same strategy over and over. So we like to put some scarcity into it by only having five or six randomly drawn cards available to buy at the same time. The popular 5/5/2 (or similar) variant from the Harbour Expansion still offers too many different cards to buy in my opinion, especially in a two player game. I like the idea to buy a desired card as soon as it pops up before someone else does it.

This game does not have any special mechanisms and can get a bit dull, especially wth two players. But it is cute to buy yourself the same things over and over again that give you the benefit in the game you want to have over the other players. Not special in every way, but entertaining and with some personality to it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6

RATING: N/A
Perhaps it is because its release date is semi-coincidental with the time my BGG adventures began, but it would appear to me that one cannot be a geek for more than a year and not hear about Machi Koro. And experience supplies the opinion that when this much buzz is happening around a game, it is one worth taking a good long look at.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Machi Koro has a couple key issues that make it hard to recommend over other similar games. At it's core, it's a gambling esque dice roller with basic deck building elements.

It's very basic and easy to understand the basics however there can be lots of confusion with the different types of buildings. With just 4 basic types it's a bit of a shame and people I've taught have trouble keeping track even after a game or two.

The biggest issue with the game is how difficult it is to strategize. The random nature of the game leads to quick games but you never really get a feel for why anything happens. As a result, the pay off for good rolls is much less satisfying. Usually players have a bit of everything and just about any roll will be middling to good for them.

For short dice rollers this is a suitable game, however be prepared for a mostly random game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
A friend's description of this game reflected my thoughts quite well: 'It's fun... if you're winning.' To me, that really is Machi Koro in a nutshell. If you get lots of coins and have all these options to buy new buildings, it's great, just like in Monopoly or Catan. But if you barely get any income, this game is a slog, and it's nigh impossible to catch up. That's ultimately what this game comes down to: the player with the most luck gets more coins, thus more opportunities to build better buildings, thus even more coins, etc., etc., while the other player(s) can barely do anything to improve their position(s). And this game is MEAN for such a cute and fluffy-looking game: those red cards are absolutely NASTY! Those rounds where you throw a certain number and three players instantly hold up their hands like hungry pigeons because they all have red buildings that equal that number. Sigh... This game just feels unfinished to me. Like, 70 or 80% of a whole game. It misses some little extra to make it more interesting to keep everyone continuously involved. Because after a while, it's pretty obvious who's winning in this game! Less may be more in some cases, but Machi Koro could definitely use some more polishing to grab my interest. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
2-5 players worth of city building, dice rolling fun. Players buy buildings from a randomized market, then throw dice to determine which of those buildings activate. Some buildings activate on any turn, while some steal money from your opponents or have other special abilities. The first to build all of their landmarks wins.

RATING: 6
A nice twist to the dice-rolling genre, which helps to reduce the luck in a pure die roll and includes a little bit of strategy in the buildings to build. Interaction is reasonably high, but replayability is definitely on the low side. 

The game is not scalable despite the box saying 2-4 players. With 2 or 3 players, the green cards are too powerful. As one may expect, the blue cards get stronger as player count increases. This suggests game development may not be complete. To make it truly scalable, the benefit to price ratio has to change with different player count and different colour cards.

Component quality is decidedly below average and the game overpriced.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Machi Koro is very much a family game, and mostly for casual players. It is also better with fewer players. I play with the recommended five 1-6 cards, five 7+ cards, and two purple cards layout, but even then found that the game is horribly chaotic with five players. I may have to add more piles to five player games, or of course (as I am now) avoid playing it with so many players.

I may have come across this game too late in my board game life, but would not mind to play it every once in a while with family, or casual friends.

Next time I want to experiment with the latout of cards available. Perhaps try five Blue, five Green, 3 Red, and two Purple cards, for a total of 15 available for purchase. I will also try having twelve piles of cards for purchase, separated by the minimum roll required to trigger the card, so there would be piles from 1-12 of random cards. In combination I may steal a mechanism from my Thunderstone Epic rules, and allow each player to take one card on top of any pile, and put it on the bottom instead, although chances are that this will mostly be used to dick with each other.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Sometimes you bump into a game, you’ve never heard of. A game which you didn’t notice in any geeklist at all. A game that, after you’ve played it, you’ll know that this will become a hit. A game that leaves you behind with a WOW! Such a game is Machi Koro. This might be the ultimate gateway game. Take a bit of Settlers of Catan (resources that will become available after rolling the dice), a splash of Dominion (card management) finished with a tuft of Carcassonne (city building), and blend it. Machi Koro has nothing to do with nor will it give you the feeling of any of these games, but it’s great in it’s own uniqueness. It’s easy to explain (each player rolls one or two dice on his turn. If the number of the building that a player owns matches the sum of the dice rolled, he gets the effect of that building. Then, with money in hand he can build a landmark or a new building. 1st player who completes 4 landmarks wins the game), has a lot of interaction in it, and it will provide you with a lot of fun. Also the artwork is very nice, and will let you smile. Too bad, this one sold out at Essen already on Friday, but I’m pretty sure a big publisher will pick this one up. Let’s hope he uses the artwork of the Japanese version, and he won’t ruin the rules.

RATING: 7
We initially liked Machi Koro as a base game and ran into some problems playing with the expansion and it's setup. Although you get a more variable setup, you sometimes get stuck with a lot of cards you can't afford or aren't useful to you. Try playing it with the new setup from the Bright Lights set, which you separate the cards into 3 decks, cards that activate from 1-6, cards that activate from 7-14 and the special purple cards. That helps a lot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The idea is fine, but the game is full to the brim with broken strategies and it's pure snowballing after the first time someone gets one of them going, as there is nothing to stop winners from winning more. The game is basically about who gets one of the broken strategies first, and then  it's a long slog until that inevitable victory becomes reality while the others are powerless to do anything about it.
It's full of bad design choices, buildings cancelling each other out in annoying ways, building combos of the same time/money cost varying widely in power, etc.

While the art is nice and cute, the production value of the "deluxe" version we played with is ATROCIOUS. It came in a dented tin box, first of all. All the cards were strongly bent in various ways, making it impossible to put them in a single deck. Many of the supplied landmark cards had the wrong card back printed on them, which made it impossible to deal them out correctly and made correct tracking of them an infuriating chore during gameplay.

Awful game, don't waste time and money on it.
